21.0 Appendix 1: Liquid Filling Case Studies

When considering what type of filler would be most appropriate for an application, several considerations
are important:

       - Ability to set up and operate in an aseptic manner for the delivery of a sterile product

       - Product characteristics

       - Container/closure to be filled

       - Mechanical capabilities of the possible filling technologies

       - Hygienic design requirements for the equipment and for the environment of the cleanroom
(especially decisions on location of components inside or outside of any barrier)

       - Good manufacturing practice (GMP) and regulatory requirements for the filling operation

       - Business needs and objectives

In addition, the filling system (and fluid pathway) should be easy to set up, operate and adjust, thereby
limiting contact with critical surfaces to the absolute minimum or best yet to no required contact at all.
When interventions are required, placement of filling system elements should enable installation and
adjustment while not compromising the quality of the environment and surrounding equipment.

The prior chapters in this Manufacturing Technology Guide outline the considerations for appropriate
design and selection of a filling technology, including key considerations in filtration, transfer of
components, environment, and utilities. This chapter presents two case studies, one for a small volume
parenteral (SVP) and one for a large volume parenteral (LVP) and steps through key decision-making in the
selection of the appropriate filling technology.

The examples are for illustrative purposes only and should not be taken as prescriptive requirements or
exclusive approaches. The products that are presented in the case studies are not intended to represent any
specific company’s product. Instead, the examples have been selected to provide the greatest possible
contrasts in decision-making. In each example, the pertinent sections of this Manufacturing Technology
Guide were converted to a decision-matrix that was used to identify the key design attributes for the filling
system that would be required for each product. When a specific attribute of the product or its
container/closure influenced the design decision, a brief explanation of how that attribute influenced
decision-making was provided.

The extensive list of requirements for the fictional products in the case studies precedes a decision matrix
and conclusion at the end of each case study that highlights the key factors that drove the technology
selection.

224 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

Table 21.1.5-1 Case 1: Anticipated Batch Sizes and Operating Practices

|Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|Batch Sizes|Batch Sizes|Batch Sizes|
|100-liter Batch Size|500,000 syringes at 0.2 mL fill volume<br>100,000 syringes at 1.0 mL fill volume<br>33,300 syringes at 3.0 mL fill volume|50,000 syringes per lane maximum each batch if using a 10-lane<br>filler (the number of cycles is needed to assess potential wear-<br>part effects on the filling pathway).|
|500-liter Batch Size|2.5 MM syringes at 0.2 mL fill<br>500,000 syringes at 1.0 mL fill volume<br>166,700 syringes at 3.0 mL fill volume|250,000 syringes per lane maximum each batch if using a 10-lane<br>filler (the number of cycles is needed to assess potential wear-<br>part effects on the filling pathway) .<br>Filler will need to be capable of long, stable run times; if using PP,<br>DP; tubing/diaphragm longevity may be an issue.|
|Campaign Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|Campaign Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|Campaign Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|
|Campaign Strategy<br>(i.e., will multiple batches be<br>campaigned to reduce<br>changeover time?)|Yes, 5 batch maximum per campaign|When considering the maximum number of batches possible per<br>week, consider system longevity and ensure that it is clear<br>whether the same primary packaging will be used for all 5<br>batches. Using different packaging may result in changeover<br>delays.|
|Length of Campaign|10 days|At line speed and batch size, this represents 5 batches with<br>flexibility for normal downtime and change of fluid path between<br>batches.|
|Filling-path Management|Replace filling-path (i.e., direct<br>product-contact parts) with each<br>batch|Filling-path must be fully disposable or be clean-in-place (CIP)/<br>sterilize-in-place (SIP)-capable.|

**Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc. 233

|Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|Container–closure<br>Management|Required to sterilize indirect product-<br>contact parts before each campaign<br>|Product utilizes the same container–closure and can be filled in<br>the same campaign; between batches, no changeover is required.|
|Environmental Monitoring|To be performed on each batch<br>within the campaign|Adequate EM materials must be located inside the filling line at<br>the start of the campaign or be able to be transferred within the<br>line between batches (e.g., rapid transfer chamber or rapid<br>transfer port (RTP) canister).<br>|
|Interventions|Aseptic Process Simulation (APS)<br>must be designed to include a<br>representative (and possibly<br>bracketed) number and type of<br>interventions during the campaign<br>|Filling machine must be designed to limit interventions and<br>should limit any interventions that would require line clearance;<br>any intervention must be designed to utilize good aseptic<br>technique.|
|Holding Times|Length of campaign must<br>accommodate standard disruptions|Consideration should be given to bulk hold times to ensure that<br>they support the campaign strategy with batches available on<br>demand but withstand possible delays incurred on changeover<br>between batches or normal process upsets.<br>|
|Batch Failure Mode|Recovery time after loss of batch to<br>be kept to a minimum|The filling system (and product supply tubing/lines) should be<br>designed both to minimize loss upon line repreparation, as well as<br>provide redundancy for backup product supply.<br>|
|APS Strategy for Campaigns|Must comply with all guidelines (e.g.,<br>EU GMP Annex 1, FDA Aseptic<br>Processing)<br>|Machine build must comply with the desired campaign strategy.|

234 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

|Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|100% IPC for Dose Weight|No|In nested fillers, 100% IPC drastically reduces the fill rate (100s<br>per hour rather than 100s per minute for non-100% IPC fills).<br>Therefore, a suitable frequency must be determined for the<br>statistical IPC. However, for expensive products, 100% IPC may<br>be more valuable at conserving product than the gains achieved<br>by higher-speed filling. (Note: If higher speed and 100% IPC are<br>both requirements, then the syringes must be de-nested and<br>again re-nested after filling and weight check are complete. This is<br>not a nest filler.)<br> <br>|
|Operating Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|Operating Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|Operating Criteria (and their influence on batch throughput)|
|Staffing Level<br>(i.e., will the facility be<br>operated 16/5, 24/5, or 24/7<br>hours per day/days per week?)|24/7|Verify whether maximum processing time per batch will be<br>accommodated in a single day and number of batches per week<br>possible with changeover.<br> <br>|
|Shutdown Allowances<br>(i.e., how many days per year<br>will the line be operated,<br>accounting for such factors as<br>shutdowns, APS, testing,<br>technical studies,<br>recapitalization, and changes?)|Two planned two-week shutdowns:<br>Winter and Summer|Verify whether the selected technology maintenance and<br>recalibration can be accommodated in the planned shutdowns.<br>Also, consider the likely duration for technology transfer for new<br>products, execution of APS, etc., relative to the number of<br>batches required to be filled each year on top of these<br>interruptions.<br> <br>|

**Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc. 235

|Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|Changeover Time<br>(i.e., what amount of time is<br>required between campaigns<br>and between batches within a<br>campaign, measured from the<br>last good container to the first<br>good container?)|32 hours between campaigns 4 hours<br>between batches within a campaign|Use these values to determine annualized throughput on the line.<br>With the time between batches for a campaign, ensure that this<br>time accounts for emptying the bulk, clearing the line, closing the<br>batch documentation, addressing any needed changeover,<br>changeover of the fluid pathway, filter-path integrity-testing,<br>closeout of EM samples. All tasks should be accounted for and<br>considered in this time, as this will influence the possible number<br>of batches within the campaign period that is planned.|
|% Yield Expected<br>(i.e., amount of scrap produced<br>on average)|98% Yield<br>2% Scrap|2–4% scrap is typical experience, inclusive of all rejection causes<br>(e.g., filter flush, filler priming, end of aseptic-fill unit operation,<br>intervention clearance).|
|% Line Efficiency<br>(i.e., during filling operations,<br>what is the operational “up<br>time” for the line?)|85% Desired|75% minimum target; More than 80% is possible, but each<br>additional percentage-point requires significant effort to achieve.<br>This parameter is often less about the equipment design and<br>more on the expertise of the operators on setup, as well as<br>appropriate sensors and detection to improve the operational<br>efficiency without down-time. Interruptions during small batches<br>will have an outsized influence on this parameter.|
|% Facility Efficiency<br>(i.e., what are the % available<br>hours in a year, during what %<br>is it expected that the line<br>operates within that range to<br>achieve the intended<br>production targets)|70 – 80%|Amount includes: needed maintenance, recalibration, shutdowns<br>for facility maintenance, and APS.|

21.1.6 Comparison of Requirements to Filling System Capabilities

Now that some of the preliminary design requirements for the product and its intended packaging have been defined, the requirements can be
compared to the filling-system capabilities and attributes as described in **Section 2.0** . Comparison of the filling system-capabilities to the product
needs, for this example, leads to the following evaluative matrix in **Table 21.1.6-1** . Refer to **Section 2.0** and **Section 4.0** for additional information.

|Viscosity Range<br>(low viscosity for this<br>product)|||||

|= available or acceptable<br> = not available or not acceptable <br>|= available or acceptable<br> = not available or not acceptable <br>|= available or acceptable<br> = not available or not acceptable <br>|= available or acceptable<br> = not available or not acceptable <br>|= available or acceptable<br> = not available or not acceptable <br>|

Based on the above chart for the Comparison of Needs to Filling System Capabilities, the product characteristics make PP, TP, and DP the leading
candidates as shear-sensitivity of the product may preclude RPP.

240 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

PP, TP, and DP are well-matched. Decision-making may come down to whether smaller volume fills will be
required in the future (ruling out DP, which is at the lower end of its capability at around 0.2 mL), or
whether single-use (SU) technology (favoring PP and DP) or CIP/SIP are preferred (favoring TP and DP). If
the foaming with the product is severe and SU technology is the desire, then PP will be the strongest
contender, as long as the number of cycles between tubing changeout is managed to be within the
capabilities of the selected tubing and that this works with the intended batch sizes.

21.1.7 Other Design Elements for Consideration

There are a number of other design decisions that will impact the layout and operation of the filling line,
such as:

       - Operator Interface with the line ( **Section 8.0** )

       - Supply of Components ( **Section 9.0** and **Section 10.0** )

       - Product Filtration, including location of filters, required number of filter elements, integrity testing
pre- and post-filling ( **Section 11.0** )

       - Filling Environment and Utilities ( **Section 14.0** and **Section 15.0** )

Generally, these decisions should be made as part of the filling-line specification process as they may affect
costs and can influence placement of equipment; however, these are generally less of a direct impact on the
selection of the filling technology itself.

|1000-liter Batch Size|250 mL fill (initial)<br>10,000 units per batch<br>50 mL – 500 mL (future)<br>20,000 to 4,000 units per batch|Note that the depyrogenation tunnel will likely be rate-limiting<br>for the line speed for this size glassware regardless of filler<br>design; therefore, increasing the number of fill heads may not<br>increase the line output.<br>1,000 to 25,000 vials per lane maximum each batch if using a 4-<br>lane filler (the number of cycles is needed to assess potential<br>wear-part effects on the filling pathway).<br>For the smallest batch sizes with the larger fill volumes, the filler<br>may have one or more station turned off. Since all of the stations<br>will not be required to operate in order to match maximum<br>tunnel line-speed, turning off one or more fill heads will conserve<br>product and reduce line losses. |Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|Shutdown Allowances<br>(i.e., how many days per year will<br>the line be operated, accounting<br>for such factors as shutdowns,<br>APS, testing, technical studies,<br>recapitalization, and changes)|Two planned two-week<br>shutdowns: Winter and Summer|Verify whether the selected technology maintenance and re-<br>calibration can be accommodated in the planned shutdowns.<br>Also, consider the likely duration for technology transfer for new<br>products, execution of APS, etc., relative to the number of<br>batches required to be filled each year on top of these<br>interruptions.|
|Changeover Time<br>(i.e., what amount of time is<br>required between campaigns and<br>between batches within a<br>campaign, measured from the last<br>good container to the first good<br>container?)|32 hours between campaigns<br>4 hours between batches within a<br>campaign|Use these values to determine annualized throughput on the<br>line.<br>With the time between batches for a campaign, ensure that this<br>time accounts for emptying the bulk, clearing the line, closing the<br>batch documentation, addressing any needed changeover,<br>changeover of the fluid-pathway, filter-path integrity testing,<br>closeout of EM samples, etc. All tasks should be accounted for<br>and considered in this time as this will influence the possible<br>number of batches within the campaign period that is planned.<br>|
|% Yield Expected<br>(i.e., amount of scrap produced on<br>average)|98% Yield<br>2% Scrap|2 – 4% scrap typical experience inclusive of all rejection causes<br>(e.g., filter flush, filler priming, end of aseptic filling-unit operation,<br>intervention clearance)|
|% Line Efficiency<br>(i.e., during filling operations what<br>is the operational “up-time” for the<br>line?)|85% Desired|75% minimum target; more than 80% is possible, but each<br>additional percentage-point requires significant effort to achieve.<br>This parameter is often less about the equipment design and<br>more on the expertise of the operators on setup, as well as<br>appropriate sensors and detection to improve the operational<br>efficiency without down-time. Interruptions during small batches<br>will have an outsized influence on this parameter.<br>|

252 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

|Attribute|Determination|Design Implication|
|---|---|---|
|% Facility Efficiency<br>(i.e., what are the % available hours<br>in a year, during what % is it<br>expected that the line operates<br>within that range to achieve the<br>intended production targets?)|70 – 80%|Amount includes needed maintenance, recalibration, shutdowns<br>for facility maintenance, APS|

**Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc. 253

21.2.6 Case 2: Comparison of Requirements to Filling System Capabilities

Now that some of the preliminary design requirements for the product and its intended packaging have been defined, the requirements can be
compared to the filling system capabilities and attributes as described in **Section 2.0** . Comparison of the filling system capabilities to the product
needs for this example leads to the following evaluative matrix in **Table 21.2.6-1** . Refer to **Section 2.0** and **Section 4.0** for additional information.

Table 21.2.6-1 Case 2: Comparison of Requirements to Filling System Capabilities

|Product Requirements|Peristaltic Pump<br>(PP)|Rotary Piston Pump<br>(RPP)|Time Pressure (TP)|Diaphragm Pump<br>(DP)|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|Filling Range||<br>pump size prohibitive for<br>the largest volumes;<br>would require multi-<br>stroke operation||<br>pump size prohibitive<br>for the largest volumes;<br>would require multi-<br>stroke operation|
|Viscosity Range (low<br>viscosity for this<br>product)|||||
|Product Compatibility<br>(proteins)||<br>not ideal for shear-<br>sensitive products|||
|Diving Needle Capable<br>to Reduce Foaming|||||
|Dynamic Fill Curve (e.g.,<br>start/stop, acceleration)<br>to Reduce Foaming|||<br>less flexibility for dynamic<br>fill curve due to pressure<br>control at vessel||

254 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

|Product Requirements|Peristaltic Pump<br>(PP)|Rotary Piston Pump<br>(RPP)|Time Pressure (TP)|Diaphragm Pump<br>(DP)|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|Range of Fill Volumes|<br>will require different<br>pump heads for<br>wide ranges of fill<br>volumes|<br>requires different pump<br>sets for different fill<br>volumes|<br>will require different orifices<br>and fluid pathway to<br>maintain accuracy across<br>the range||
|Drip Control|||<br>viscosity higher than water<br>will reduce likelihood to<br>drip, and rapid closure of<br>pinch-valve is likely to be<br>sufficient||
|Dosing Accuracy||<br>very accurate, but<br>volume of fill<br>contraindicates this<br>technology|||
|Dosing Accuracy<br>Stability Across Run|<br>100% IPC planned|<br>statistical IPC adequate,<br>dosing accuracy very<br>stable|<br>100% IPC planned|<br>100% IPC planned|

**Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc. 255

|Product Requirements|Peristaltic Pump<br>(PP)|Rotary Piston Pump<br>(RPP)|Time Pressure (TP)|Diaphragm Pump<br>(DP)|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|Clean-In-Place<br>(CIP)/Sterilize-In-Place<br>(SIP) Execution|<br>rare (but possible);<br>often SU instead||||
|Stability Over Long<br>Batches/Number of<br>Cycles|<br>hose wear a<br>detriment, but<br>relatively modest<br>batch sizes make<br>this suitable||<br>hose wear at pinch point<br>can be a minor<br>consideration; processor<br>required for varying head<br>pressure|<br>diaphragm wear can be<br>a minor consideration<br>but usually only after<br>excessive operation|
|Campaign Compatibility|<br>hose wear must be<br>considered|<br>filling equipment can be<br>replaced|<br>single-point hose-wear<br>must be considered|<br>diaphragm-wear must<br>be considered|
|Execution Outside<br>Isolator or Restricted<br>Access Barrier System<br>(RABS)|||||
|Speed of Setup<br>Changeover||<br>requires aseptic setup or<br>CIP/SIP|<br>requires aseptic setup or<br>CIP/SIP, but fluid path is<br>reduced compared to RPP|<br>requires aseptic setup,<br>CIP/SIP, or SU (which is<br>the fastest changeover)|
|= available or acceptable<br>= not available or not acceptable|= available or acceptable<br>= not available or not acceptable|= available or acceptable<br>= not available or not acceptable|= available or acceptable<br>= not available or not acceptable|= available or acceptable<br>= not available or not acceptable|

256 **Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc.

Based on the above chart, the product characteristics make PP and TP the leading candidates, as planned fill
volumes and shear-sensitivity of the product may preclude RPP (both volume and shear) and DP (volume
alone).

PP and TP are well-matched. Decision-making may come down to whether SU technology (favoring PP) or
CIP/SIP are preferred (favoring TP). That decision may be driven by the changeover time with each, as the
modest batch sizes will result in frequent changeover of the fluid pathway within the campaigns. If the
foaming with the product is severe and SU technology is the desire, then PP will be the strongest contender
if the cost of SU is not prohibitive based on the planned number of batches.

21.2.7 Other Design Elements for Consideration

There are a number of other design decisions that will impact the layout and operation of the filling line,
such as:

       - Operator Interface with the line ( **Section 8.0** )

       - Supply of Components ( **Section 9.0** and **Section 10.0** )

       - Product Filtration, including location of filters, required number of filter elements, integrity-testing
pre- and post-filling ( **Section 11.0** )

       - Filling Environment and Utilities ( **Section 14.0** and **Section 15.0** )

Generally, these decisions should be made as part of the filling-line specification process as they may affect
costs and can influence placement of equipment; however, these are generally less of a direct impact on the
selection of the filling technology itself.

**Manufacturing Technology Guide No. 1** © 2025 Parenteral Drug Association, Inc. 257

About PDA Technical Documents

PDA Technical Documents are global consensus documents, prepared by member-driven Teams (listed on
inside front cover) comprised of content experts, including scientists and engineers working in the
pharmaceutical/biopharmaceutical industry, regulatory authorities and academia. The viewpoints and
strategies presented in this document are those of the authors as independent subject matter experts and do
not necessarily represent the policies and practices of their respective companies.

While in development, PDA technical documents are subjected to a global review by PDA members and
other topic-specific experts, often including regulatory officials. Comments from the global review are then
considered by the authoring Team during the preparation of the final working draft. The level of expertise of
the Team and those participating in the global review ensure a broad perspective reflecting best thinking and
practices currently available.

The final working draft is next reviewed by the PDA Advisory Board(s) that sanctioned the project. PDA’s
Advisory Boards are: Science Advisory Board, Advanced Therapy Medicinal Products Advisory Board,
Biopharmaceutical Advisory Board, and Regulatory Affairs and Quality Advisory Board. Following this
stage of review, the PDA Board of Directors conducts the final review and determines whether or not to
publish the document as an official PDA Technical Document.

While PDA goes to great lengths to ensure each Technical Document is of the highest quality, all readers are
encouraged to contact PDA about any scientific, technical, or regulatory inaccuracies, discrepancies, or
mistakes that might be found in any of the documents. Readers can email PDA at: TRfeedback@pda.org.
